SCOPE OP THESIS 










The woi?d fiscal refeT’s specifically to -onblic finance 

which concerns the income and outgo of government. In 

this thesis, ?.t is nroposed to survey briefly the 

grov^th of the Alberta 'lovernment^s financial dealings 

from the year of its inception, 1905, to the present 

day; from the days when its total annual receints 

(I) 

amounted to a mere $1,890,311 and it ended i^he year 

with a balance of $412,398 in cold cash doTO to the 

expansi.ve days of 1948 when the government budgetted 

nonchalantly for a deficit of $6,306,738 on top of an 

(2) 

expected revenue of $45,230,595, Since public finance 
is on tbe borderline between econom_ics and political 
science, this account will necessarily, but concisely, 
give some attention to these fields. The problem of 
public debt has been a major one in Albertats short 
history and the growth of this debt wil'’ he examined 
in the liffht of Alberta’s unfortunate repudiation in 
il936_ and its subsequent debt reorganization program. 
Much space will be devoted to a. study of Dominion- 
Provincial relations, that thorny problem which be¬ 
came so urgent in the depression thirties; and which 
now, even after an expensive investigation by a Royal 
Commission and lengthy Dominion-Provincial Confer¬ 
ences, has only been met by temporary and makeshift 













2 


meRsures, This question concerns 5.tself basically 
v/ith an equitable reallocation of the rights and res¬ 
ponsibilities of each government and a satisfactory 
settlement of their financial relations. Finally, a 
brief view will be taken of Alberta’s immensely im- 
p-»^oved 1948 financial position due in a large meastTre 
to the exigencies of a long and devastating wao^ re¬ 
cently concluded and,to tbme extent, to the almost 
feverish development by outside capital of the pro¬ 
vince’s great natural resources.

Population 

The population of the district which subseouently 
became Alberta was estimated to be 73,000 in 1901,but, 
according to the census of 1906, it had gromTi to 185,185 
The p-rowth of ponulation between 1905 and 1914 w^^.s so 
great as to be alm^ost without parallel in modern history 
In 1911 it reached 301,273 but the rate of increase 
then slowed dov/n, paused during the Ore at War and only 
resumed intermittently atter that. This repid p-rowth of 
population was fostered by the Mo^'nion whoee avowed 
intention at Confederation was to bring all of ‘P-n-itisb 
ivTorth Ame'^^.ca under one government, to weld al^ this 
territory into a strong political entity, Tn 1870, it 
acquired the Northwest Territories and Runert’s Land, 
and then made colonization and settlement a nrominent 
feature of its national policy. Its policy was to briner 
set-rlers in, so that they 1 ndirecUyv/ould *'give lemlti- 
mate occupation, without excessive duties, on a legiti¬ 
mate business basis, to the mechanics and artisans in 
( 8 ) 

Eastern Canada.” Every even numbered section of land 

was open for homestead entry and the immivgrants moved 
in and took their nick of the land rep^ardless of soil.

it, but it cannot be denied that more caution and 
foresight would have prevented, the accumulation of 
so much deadweight debt at this tim.e..

for a population of 592,000 can be considered from 

various angles. In a. sovereign state, where the 

government can control its ciir^encTT- ful].y, a debt of 

twice the national income is not too large to be 

considered alarming says Groves in his ”Publie 

Financing” but this rule of thumb cannot be applied 

to Alberta since it has no control over currency or 

cred5.t*0ne must Instead take Into account income and 

the relation of the debt charges to that income. The 

Alberta government ks incom.e was not elastic. The

the tax system^inelastic and nnprooressive since

Another disquieting fact about Alberta’s indebtedness 

(:^ 0 ) 

was the fact that the debt charges formed 24^ of 
the provincial expenditure. Debt charges are an uncon¬ 
trollable item of expenditure and are disastrous 
to a province’s economy in depression times. Unfor¬ 
tunately by 1921, Albert’s ordinary expenditure was 
tending more and more to services that were relative¬ 
ly difficult to adjust doTOwards--social and educa¬ 
tional services besides the increasingly oppressive 
debt charges*
